Jamison Brereton

67 (779)
Soma Pavama╠Дna [or, according to Anukraman╠г─▒╠Д, Soma Pavama╠Дna 1тАУ9, 13тАУ22, 28тАУ30; Soma Pavama╠Дna or Pu╠Дsan ╠г 10тАУ12; Agni 23, 24; Agni or Savitar 25; Agni or Agni and Savitar 26; Agni or the All Gods 27; Praise of the Student of the Pavama╠Дn─▒╠Д Verses 31тАУ32]
Bharadv─Бja 1тАУ3, Ka┼Ыyapa 4тАУ6, Gotama 7тАУ9, Atri 10тАУ12, Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra 13тАУ15, Jamadagni 16тАУ18, Vasiс╣гс╣нha 19тАУ21, Pavitra ─Ас╣Еgirasa or Vasiс╣гtha or both 22тАУ32
32 verses: g─Бyatr─л, except dvipad─Б g─Бyatr─л 16тАУ18, anuс╣гс╣нubh 27, 31тАУ32, pura├╝с╣гс╣Зih 30, arranged in trcas through verse 21. ╠е

This final hymn of the IXth Maс╣Зс╕Нala g─Бyatr─л collection is, according to the Anukramaс╣З─л, the product of at least seven different poets, most of them found ing poets of the Family Books of the R╠еgveda and all eponymous ancestors of the most prominent gotras of later timesтАФeach responsible for a single tr╠еca, with the last eleven verses assigned either to yet another poet or to Vasiс╣гс╣нha, the poet of the immediately preceding tr╠еca.

It has been convincingly argued by Proferes (2007: 10) that this structure shows that the separate lineage rites were already being collected into a single pan-─Аrya ritual during the R╠еgvedic period and that this com posite hymn was a conscious attempt to bring the various clans under one ritual umbrella. He argues that the final two verses (31тАУ32) were added by the redactors or compilers and celebrate this ecumenical project, by declaring that the assemblage is greater and more powerful than the sum of its parts.

Certainly its parts are quite various. The first twenty-one verses, attributed to the various clan-poets, are for the most part conventional Soma Pavam─Бna verses, and the tr╠еcas for the most part exhibit some internal cohesion.

The single unconventional tr╠еca consists of verses 10тАУ12, attributed to Atri, in which the god P┼лс╣гan dominates. P┼лс╣гan otherwise scarcely appears in the IXth Maс╣Зс╕Нala, has little or no connection with soma elsewhere (gruel is his favored meal), and barely appears in the Atri Maс╣Зс╕Нala (V) except in lists of other gods. The reason for his intrusion here is unclear.

Verses 22тАУ27 form a self-contained purificatory spell, calling on various gods, including Soma, Agni, and Savitar, in addition to groups of gods, to purify us with their own characteristic instruments. There follow two verses (28тАУ29) that return to the soma theme, and then the strangest verse of all (30). The interpretation of this verse is much disputed, but it appears to refer to the myth of the disappearance of the ritual Agni and urges Soma to bring him back, along with a, or the, rat, which may be a reference to the god Rudra, whose animal victim in later ritual is a rat. But this is only a guess.

The last two supplementary verses (31тАУ32) were discussed above.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the structure and authorship of this hymn and their implications for RVic studies more generally, see published introduction. as well as Oldenberg

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the place of these long hymns in the structure of the maс╣Зс╕Нala, see published introduction. to IX.61. All of these hymns consist of collections of tс╣Ыcas of varying degrees of cohesion. In fact, it is surprising how few tс╣Ыcas display a real attempt at thematic or lexical unity, and what they do show is often simply the byproduct of the fact that both the lexicon and thematic preoccupations of Maс╣Зс╕Нala IX are comparatively limited and so similar words and themes are not unlikely to show up in adjacent verses. This lack of unity contrasts, I think (this is my unsystematic and anecdotal impression) with collections of tс╣Ыcas in other maс╣Зс╕Нalas, notably VIII.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Attributed to Bharadv─Бja, the tс╣Ыca shows elementary unity by positoning tv├бm at the beginning of each vs. ### 01 Grassmann derives dh─Бray├║- from dh─Б╠Бr─Б- тАШstreamтАЩ and glosses тАШstr├╢mendтАЩ, an idea that goes back to S─Бyaс╣Зa. Geldner follows suit (тАЬder hervorsprudelndeтАЭ), though in n. 1a he entertains a derivation from тИЪdhс╣Ы, which underlies RenouтАЩs тАЬle mainteneurтАЭ (see his n.) I also think that тИЪdhс╣Ы is the correct etymon. There would be no reason to shorten the final of dh─Б╠Бr─Б- (though dh─Б╠Бra-p┼лta- тАШpurified by streamsтАЩ (?) and dh─Бra-v─Бk├б- тАШrecitation for the streamsтАЩ (?) do give me pause), while there is a well-established relationship between -─Б╠Ж-y├б-(ti) verbs and - ─Б╠Ж-y├║- adjectives (type devay├б- / devay├║-). Though it has a different accent and a different functional profile, dh─Б╠Бr├бyati is a very common verb, and it is easy to imagine the creation of a -y├║- nominal to that stem. However, if it is derived from dh─Б╠Бr─Б- тАШstreamтАЩ, the sense should not be of the type given by Grassmann and Geldner, but rather тАШseeking streamsтАЩ, as is standard with -yuformations to nouns. The idea would be that the soma, once pressed, goes forth to seek the ritual waters.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Attributed to Bharadv─Бja, the tс╣Ыca shows elementary unity by positoning tv├бm at the beginning of each vs. ### 01 Grassmann derives dh─Бray├║- from dh─Б╠Бr─Б- тАШstreamтАЩ and glosses тАШstr├╢mendтАЩ, an idea that goes back to S─Бyaс╣Зa. Geldner follows suit (тАЬder hervorsprudelndeтАЭ), though in n. 1a he entertains a derivation from тИЪdhс╣Ы, which underlies RenouтАЩs тАЬle mainteneurтАЭ (see his n.) I also think that тИЪdhс╣Ы is the correct etymon. There would be no reason to shorten the final of dh─Б╠Бr─Б- (though dh─Б╠Бra-p┼лta- тАШpurified by streamsтАЩ (?) and dh─Бra-v─Бk├б- тАШrecitation for the streamsтАЩ (?) do give me pause), while there is a well-established relationship between -─Б╠Ж-y├б-(ti) verbs and - ─Б╠Ж-y├║- adjectives (type devay├б- / devay├║-). Though it has a different accent and a different functional profile, dh─Б╠Бr├бyati is a very common verb, and it is easy to imagine the creation of a -y├║- nominal to that stem. However, if it is derived from dh─Б╠Бr─Б- тАШstreamтАЩ, the sense should not be of the type given by Grassmann and Geldner, but rather тАШseeking streamsтАЩ, as is standard with -yuformations to nouns. The idea would be that the soma, once pressed, goes forth to seek the ritual waters.

тАЬThe excellent juice of the Soma dropping (into the vessels) poured forth by men of old, the goer (to Indra), is filtered for Indra the goer.тАЭ

English translation:

тАЬMay the goat-borne P┼лс╣гan in all his paths be our protector; may he bestow maidens on us.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Jamison Brereton

P┼лс╣гan whose horses are goats is our helper on every journey.
тАУ He will give us a share in maidens.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The published translation presents the journeys as ours, reading naс╕е with both avit─Б╠Бand y─Б╠Бmani-y─Бmani. Geldner and Renou instead assume the journeys are P┼лс╣гanтАЩs: e.g., тАЬUnser G├╢nner ist P┼лс╣гan, der auf jeder Ausfahrt B├╢cke als Rosse hat.тАЭ Since one hymn devoted to P┼лс╣гan, I.42, is almost entirely devoted to P┼лс╣гanтАЩs protection of us on the journey and on the path and in the short P┼лс╣гan cycle in VI (VI.53-58) the god is several times asked to lead or direct us (e.g., VI.53.2) and to clear paths for us (VI.53.4, 54.1-2), I think it likely that the focus here is on our journeys.

10-12 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

This, the Atri tс╣Ыca, shows very tight cohesion. To begin with, P┼лс╣гan, under his name (vs. 10) or characteristic epithets (kapard├нn- vs. 11, ─Б╠Бghс╣Ыс╣Зi- vs. 12), appears in every vs., in particular as the recipient of the pressed and purified soma in vss. 11-12. Since, as noted in the published introduction, P┼лс╣гan is rarely found in IX, devoting a tс╣Ыca to him here is striking, and the assumption that he wants soma is esp. anomalous, since in the hymn devoted to Indra and P┼лс╣гan (VI.57) IndraтАЩs desire for soma is explicitly contrasted with P┼лс╣гanтАЩs for porridge (VI.57.2).

More evidence for strong cohesion: the third p─Бda of each vs. is a refrain: ─Б╠Бbhakс╣гat kany─Б╠Аsu naс╕е тАЬHe [=P┼лс╣гan] will give us a share in maidens.тАЭ The connection of this refrain with soma is, to say the least, not straightforward. Geldner (n. 10-12) suggests that the idea is that P┼лс╣гan, as a thank-you for the soma, will give maidens as compensation for the poet (Dichtersold) or as a guest-gift (Gastgeschenk). This is certainly possible, though the quid pro quo isnтАЩt evident to me in the text; nonetheless it conforms to the d─Бnastuti concept.

Ober refines this somewhat by identifying the maidens as brides (e.g., I.320), but at least in his vol. II he takes Soma as the subject of the refrain (тАЬ[D]er [Soma] gew├дhre uns Anteil an den JungfrauenтАЭ; II.51 n. 240). Simply on the basis of rhetorical structure, this seems unlikely: in the vs. containing the first appearance of the refrain (10) there is not even indirect reference to Soma; the only possible subject is P┼лс╣гan, and there is unlikely to be a switch in subject in the refrain in the following two vss. By contrast, Grassmann identifies the maidens here as daughters, presumbly implying that this expression is a twist on the тАЬgive us sonsтАЭ wish so often expressed. Though I appreciate GrassmannтАЩs attempt to save P┼лс╣гan from being a pimp, I doubt that any Rigvedin would wish [at least out loud] for a passel of daughters The last two vss. of the tс╣Ыca are simple variants of each other. Both begin with ay├бm referring to soma; both contain a datival expression identifying P┼лс╣гan as the recipient of the soma; their b p─Бdas both begin ghс╣Ыt├бс╣Г n├б pavate, with a disyllable qualifiying ghс╣Ыt├бm following.

English translation:

тАЬThis Soma flows to P┼лс╣гan, who wears a beautiful tiara like exhilarating gh─л; may he bestow maidens on us.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Who wears a beautiful tiara: kapardine = kaly─Бс╣Зa mukuс╣нavate; this also means, having hair braided like a cowrie shell (kaparda)

Jamison Brereton

This Soma here purifies himself, honeyed like ghee, for the one with braided hair [=P┼лс╣гan].
тАУ He will give us a share in maidens.

Jamison Brereton Notes

kapard├нn- is used of P┼лс╣гan in VI.55.2, though it is also applied to a few other gods in the RV, notably Rudra (I.114.1, 5).

Jamison Brereton Notes

This, the Atri tс╣Ыca, shows very tight cohesion. To begin with, P┼лс╣гan, under his name (vs. 10) or characteristic epithets (kapard├нn- vs. 11, ─Б╠Бghс╣Ыс╣Зi- vs. 12), appears in every vs., in particular as the recipient of the pressed and purified soma in vss. 11-12. Since, as noted in the published introduction, P┼лс╣гan is rarely found in IX, devoting a tс╣Ыca to him here is striking, and the assumption that he wants soma is esp. anomalous, since in the hymn devoted to Indra and P┼лс╣гan (VI.57) IndraтАЩs desire for soma is explicitly contrasted with P┼лс╣гanтАЩs for porridge (VI.57.2).

More evidence for strong cohesion: the third p─Бda of each vs. is a refrain: ─Б╠Бbhakс╣гat kany─Б╠Аsu naс╕е тАЬHe [=P┼лс╣гan] will give us a share in maidens.тАЭ The connection of this refrain with soma is, to say the least, not straightforward. Geldner (n. 10-12) suggests that the idea is that P┼лс╣гan, as a thank-you for the soma, will give maidens as compensation for the poet (Dichtersold) or as a guest-gift (Gastgeschenk). This is certainly possible, though the quid pro quo isnтАЩt evident to me in the text; nonetheless it conforms to the d─Бnastuti concept.

Ober refines this somewhat by identifying the maidens as brides (e.g., I.320), but at least in his vol. II he takes Soma as the subject of the refrain (тАЬ[D]er [Soma] gew├дhre uns Anteil an den JungfrauenтАЭ; II.51 n. 240). Simply on the basis of rhetorical structure, this seems unlikely: in the vs. containing the first appearance of the refrain (10) there is not even indirect reference to Soma; the only possible subject is P┼лс╣гan, and there is unlikely to be a switch in subject in the refrain in the following two vss. By contrast, Grassmann identifies the maidens here as daughters, presumbly implying that this expression is a twist on the тАЬgive us sonsтАЭ wish so often expressed. Though I appreciate GrassmannтАЩs attempt to save P┼лс╣гan from being a pimp, I doubt that any Rigvedin would wish [at least out loud] for a passel of daughters The last two vss. of the tс╣Ыca are simple variants of each other. Both begin with ay├бm referring to soma; both contain a datival expression identifying P┼лс╣гan as the recipient of the soma; their b p─Бdas both begin ghс╣Ыt├бс╣Г n├б pavate, with a disyllable qualifiying ghс╣Ыt├бm following.

English translation:

тАЬThis Soma poured for you, shining (P┼лс╣гan) flows like pure gh─л; may he bestow maidens on us.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

This pressed soma here purifies himself, gleaming like ghee, for you, glowing one.
тАУ He will give us a share in maidens.

Jamison Brereton Notes

─Б╠Бghс╣Ыс╣Зi- is an epithet exclusive to P┼лс╣гan. On the word, see comm. ad VI.53.3.

That P┼лс╣гan is addressed in the 2nd ps. here, while the refrain remains in the 3rd ps., might be taken as evidence for OberтАЩs identification of Soma as the subject of the refrain - since soma is in the 3rd ps. in this vs. However, refrains tend to operate in syntactic independence from their vss., and, as I argued above, once the refrain is set, it is unlikely to change referents.

10-12 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

This, the Atri tс╣Ыca, shows very tight cohesion. To begin with, P┼лс╣гan, under his name (vs. 10) or characteristic epithets (kapard├нn- vs. 11, ─Б╠Бghс╣Ыс╣Зi- vs. 12), appears in every vs., in particular as the recipient of the pressed and purified soma in vss. 11-12. Since, as noted in the published introduction, P┼лс╣гan is rarely found in IX, devoting a tс╣Ыca to him here is striking, and the assumption that he wants soma is esp. anomalous, since in the hymn devoted to Indra and P┼лс╣гan (VI.57) IndraтАЩs desire for soma is explicitly contrasted with P┼лс╣гanтАЩs for porridge (VI.57.2).

More evidence for strong cohesion: the third p─Бda of each vs. is a refrain: ─Б╠Бbhakс╣гat kany─Б╠Аsu naс╕е тАЬHe [=P┼лс╣гan] will give us a share in maidens.тАЭ The connection of this refrain with soma is, to say the least, not straightforward. Geldner (n. 10-12) suggests that the idea is that P┼лс╣гan, as a thank-you for the soma, will give maidens as compensation for the poet (Dichtersold) or as a guest-gift (Gastgeschenk). This is certainly possible, though the quid pro quo isnтАЩt evident to me in the text; nonetheless it conforms to the d─Бnastuti concept.

Ober refines this somewhat by identifying the maidens as brides (e.g., I.320), but at least in his vol. II he takes Soma as the subject of the refrain (тАЬ[D]er [Soma] gew├дhre uns Anteil an den JungfrauenтАЭ; II.51 n. 240). Simply on the basis of rhetorical structure, this seems unlikely: in the vs. containing the first appearance of the refrain (10) there is not even indirect reference to Soma; the only possible subject is P┼лс╣гan, and there is unlikely to be a switch in subject in the refrain in the following two vss. By contrast, Grassmann identifies the maidens here as daughters, presumbly implying that this expression is a twist on the тАЬgive us sonsтАЭ wish so often expressed. Though I appreciate GrassmannтАЩs attempt to save P┼лс╣гan from being a pimp, I doubt that any Rigvedin would wish [at least out loud] for a passel of daughters The last two vss. of the tс╣Ыca are simple variants of each other. Both begin with ay├бm referring to soma; both contain a datival expression identifying P┼лс╣гan as the recipient of the soma; their b p─Бdas both begin ghс╣Ыt├бс╣Г n├б pavate, with a disyllable qualifiying ghс╣Ыt├бm following.

This Soma juice, O glowing God, flows like pure oil, effused for thee:
He shall give us our share of maids.

Geldner

Grassmann

English translation:

тАЬSoma, genitive rator of the praises of the wise, flow in a stream; you are the dispenser of treasure among the gods.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Genitive rator of the praises of the wise: Or, in the midst of the wise, it is you who does genitive rate speech

Jamison Brereton

As child of the speech of the poets, o Soma, purify yourself in a stream. You are the one among the gods who establishes treasure.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Soma is obviously тАЬchild of the speech of the poetsтАЭ (v─Бc├│ jant├║с╕е kav─лn─Б╠Бm) because ritual speech sets in motion the preparation of soma.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Little evidence of unity in the Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra tс╣Ыca, though the falcon (┼Ыyen├б-) as image of Soma occurs in both 14 and 15. More generally, both 14 and 15 depict the rapid and dramatic movement of soma into the ritual receptacles.

English translation:

тАЬHe has flowed to the vessels as a falcon enters its nest and enters the wooden (vessels) with a cry.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

He runs into the tubs; the falcon plunges through the armor
toward the cups, roaring constantly.

Jamison Brereton Notes

What is the тАЬarmorтАЭ (v├бrma) that soma тАЬplunges throughтАЭ (v├н g─Бhate)? Geldner (n.

14ab) suggests that the image is of a warrior clothing himself in armor, that is, the wooden cup, but he doesnтАЩt construe v├бrma with the verb, but takes it as loosely descriptive with a verb used absolutely (тАЬer taucht in seinen Panzer unterтАЭ). Renou also thinks the v├бrma refers to the cup (тАЬla paroi [wall] de la cuveтАЭ acdg. to his n.), but has the courage to construe it with the verb: тАЬil plonge dans (le r├йcipient, sa) cuirasse.тАЭ But v├н is not тАЬdans.тАЭ I think it refers instead to the filter, whose fleece both represents his armor and a substance that soma must get through, hence the slightly off-balance image. Note first of all that the same verb, though with different preverb, is used precisely with the filter a few vss. later in this hymn:

13-15 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

Little evidence of unity in the Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra tс╣Ыca, though the falcon (┼Ыyen├б-) as image of Soma occurs in both 14 and 15. More generally, both 14 and 15 depict the rapid and dramatic movement of soma into the ritual receptacles.

Griffith

The Falcon dips within the jars: he wrap.him in his robe and goes
Loud roaring to the vats of wood.

тАЬYour juice, Soma, spreads around when effused into the vessel as a swift falcon rushes along.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Your sap, Soma, when pressed, has surged forth and around into the tub.
Like a falcon launched in flight, it rushes. с│У

Jamison Brereton Notes

Little evidence of unity in the Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra tс╣Ыca, though the falcon (┼Ыyen├б-) as image of Soma occurs in both 14 and 15. More generally, both 14 and 15 depict the rapid and dramatic movement of soma into the ritual receptacles.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This tс╣Ыca, ascribed to Jamadagni, is unifed first and foremost by its meter, Dvipad─Б G─Бyatr─л, the only representative of this meter in the hymn - and in fact in all of the RV (see Arnold p. 244). There is no particular unity in lexicon (though note mand├бyan 16a and mad├нntamaс╕е 18a), but the tс╣Ыca does mention the two gods who receive the first soma oblation, Indra (16) and V─Бyu (18).

Jamison Brereton Notes

This tс╣Ыca, ascribed to Jamadagni, is unifed first and foremost by its meter, Dvipad─Б G─Бyatr─л, the only representative of this meter in the hymn - and in fact in all of the RV (see Arnold p. 244). There is no particular unity in lexicon (though note mand├бyan 16a and mad├нntamaс╕е 18a), but the tс╣Ыca does mention the two gods who receive the first soma oblation, Indra (16) and V─Бyu (18).

Jamison Brereton Notes

This is the last tс╣Ыca of the hymn, attributed to Vasiс╣гс╣нha. The 1st two vss. are variants of each, couched in 2nd and 3rd ps. respectively: their first p─Бdas differ only in their initial disyllable, with the rest identical: тАж tunn├│ abh├нс╣гс╣нutaс╕е; their second p─Бdas both depict the journey to and across the filter, both beginning pav├нtram. The third vs. stands apart, though the identification of Soma as тАШdemon-smasherтАЩ in 20c is thematically linked to the plea in 21 that Soma тАЬsmash awayтАЭ peril.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬMay that purified Soma, the beholder of all, who is the purifier, purify us now by his purifying (might).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Yajus. 19.42

Jamison Brereton

The one who purifies himself through our filter today, the limitless one who is the purifier, let him purify us.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The nominal rel. cl. y├бс╕е pot─Б╠Бseems to be another ex. of the embedded, pseudoizafe construction discussed elsewhere, though in this case there is a resumptive pronoun beginning the continuation of the main cl. The structure is: ab beg. of main cl. marked by s├бс╕е, no verb; c rel. cl. y├бс╕е pot─Б╠Б, followed by rest of main cl., with verb, but introduced by a repetition of s├бс╕е: s├б pun─Бtu naс╕е, or, more schematically: ab s├б NOMINATIVE NP / C. y├бс╕е NOM. NP, s├б VERB with the two s├б hunks together forming the main cl. A sort of hybrid construction.

In the published translation I take pav├нtreс╣Зa in b with p├бvam─Бnaс╕е in a, unlike Geldner and Renou, who construe it with pun─Бtu in c (e.g., тАЬquтАЩil nous clarifie avec le filtreтАЭ). Their interpr. is supported by the INSTR. pun─лhi naс╕е constr. in 23-24, 26 (and variant in 25), and since I no longer consider y├бс╕е pot─Б╠Бan embedded rel. clause and therefore have no need to consider ab entirely separate from the resumptive s├б phrase in c, there is no syntactic obstacle to this interpr. IтАЩd be inclined, however, to read pav├нtreс╣Зa with both: тАЬThe one who purifies himself through our filter (/purifier) today, the limitless one who is the purifier, with the filter (/purifier) let him purify us.тАЭ The agent noun pot├бr- (also p├│tar-) as if to an aniс╣н root is surprising, esp. in juxtaposition to the instrument noun pav├нtra- and, a few vss. later, the god savit├бr- to the parallel root тИЪs┼л. We would of course expect *pavit├бr- – a form we almost get in RVic pav─лt├бr- and do get in AV pavit├бr-. The problem is identical to that posed by the priestly title h├│tar-, derived from тИЪhv─Б / h┼л тАШinvokeтАЩ, so we expect *h├бvitar- – although in that instance interference from the likewise ritual verb тИЪhu тАШpour, libateтАЩ, whose agent noun should properly be h├│tar-, helps explain the discrepancy. AiG II.2. 672 explains p├│tar- by suggesting that it is an inherited word and no longer closely tied to the verb from which it was originally derived. This seems backwards to me - wouldnтАЩt inherited and isolated words be more likely to maintain their expected phonological shape? And, judging from this passage, pot├бr- (so accented) has not lost its connection with тИЪp┼л. However, a few pp. later (AiG II.2.676) the much more plausible scenario is proposed, that pot├бr- / p├│tar- has followed h├│tar-, which owes its shape to the interference just noted, and stot├бr- тАШpraiserтАЩ.

Sim. EWA s.v. p├│tar-.

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬPurify, Agni, our divine (body) with your purifying (light), which is diffused in the midst of the light.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Divine body: divine, because it increases, viz., sons; the light is the light of the sun, lightning

Jamison Brereton

The filter that is stretched out here within your flame, o Agni,
and the sacred formulationтАФwith that purify us.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The image of the filter stretched out in AgniтАЩs flame is striking, but I donтАЩt know exactly what the picture is meant to be - in contemplating the fire do we have a vision of a purifying apparatus? Or is the expression simply a fancy way of saying тАЬthe purifier that is your flameтАЭ? The beginning of the next vs., with pav├нtram arciv├бt, would support the latter suggestions.

Geldner takes br├бhma as obj. of pun─лhi: тАЬтАж mit der [=pav├нtram] l├дutere unsere feierliche Rede.тАЭ But 1) this would break the pattern of pun─Бtu / pun─лhi naс╕е тАЬpurify usтАЭ in vss. 22, 24, 26) by demoting naс╕е from object; 2) br├бhma as a means of purification is found in the next vs. in the cmpd brahmasava├нс╕е. The fairly strict rhetorical patterning of these vss. therefore imposes (at least to my view) an instrument-of-means reading on br├бhma, and I see no reason why it canтАЩt be part of the definitional preposed rel. cl. y├бd te тАЬwhat is your тАж,тАЭ parallel to pav├нtram. y├бd would be appropriate to both, since they are both neut., and they are then both picked up by instr. t├йna in c. That br├бhma has been postponed till the beginning of c is not surprising, given the long NP containing pav├нtram. The new cl. begins mid-p─Бda with t├йna; since s├б/t├бm forms regularly take init. position in p─Бda / clause, the mid-p─Бda position here suggests that it begins a new cl. and br├бhma is not part of it. Like Geldner, Renou makes br├бhma somehow oblique, but I donтАЩt really understand what heтАЩs trying to convey: тАЬclarifies en notre Formule.тАЭ

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

English translation:

тАЬPurify us, Agni, with your purifying (light) which possesses the light; purify us with the effusion of the Soma.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Your filter of flames, AgniтАФwith that purify us;
with the impulsions of sacred formulations purify us.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. has both physical and conceptual purifying instruments: the flame-sieve of 23 and the br├бhman- also introduced in 23, which, in the cmpd brahma-sav├б-, also provides a transition to Savitar and his impulsions in vs. 25.

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

And by libations poured to thee.

English translation:

тАЬShining (Soma), urger of all things, purify me by both, by your purifying (light) and by the effusion (of the Soma); purify me in every wayтАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Yajus. 19.43; your three…forms: i.e., fire, wind and sun

Jamison Brereton

With both, god Savitar, with the filter and with your impulsion
purify me all about.

Jamison Brereton Notes

In addition to the introduction of Savitar, we also get a slight rearrangement in word order. The conjoined phrase pav├нtreс╣Зa sav├йna ca would not fit in the slot right before the impv. in c, a position it occupies in 23c, 24b, 24c, 26c, so the final enclitic naс╕е is converted to a full prn. m─Б╠Бm and placed in initial position. This prn. also has to be read as distracted m├б─Бm to achieve 8 syllables. I wonder why the poet didnтАЩt just use asm─Б╠Бn, which would fit the meter and better match naс╕е. One of the only instances in which I think I could compose the vs. better than the poet - though тАЬmeтАЭ (both tonic m─Б╠Бm and enclitic m─Б) serves as insistent obj. in vs. 27.

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

English translation:

тАЬPurify us, shining Agni Pavam─Бna, urger of all things, with your three most mighty powerful forms.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

With the three purify usтАФyou, god Savitar, with your greatest
(impulsions), you, Soma, through your greatest domains,
and you, Agni, with your skills.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Initial tribh├нс╕е matches initial ubh─Б╠Бbhy─Бm in 25a. Since ubh─Б╠Бbhy─Бm was further specified by a bipartite NP pav├нtreс╣Зa sav├йna ca (25b), I would like to see three instr. in this vs., each correlated with one of the three gods mentioned. And this is how I have rendered it, with v├бrс╣гiс╣гс╣нhaiс╕е connected to Savitar (and Soma), dh─Б╠Бmabhiс╕е with Soma, and d├бkс╣гaiс╕е with Agni. By contrast, Geldner and Renou construe v├бrс╣гiс╣гс╣нhaiс╕е with dh─Б╠Бmabhiс╕е, which they consider to be of three types (that is, тАЬwith the three highest dh─Б╠Бmans тАжтАЭ; Renou тАЬavec les trois positions les plus ├йminentesтАЭ) - although in their notes both come close to espousing a position close to mine. Possibly in their favor is the fact that soma is unaccented in b, and if v├бrс╣гiс╣гс╣нhaiс╕е is followed by a sub-clausal break, the voc. might (or might not) have been accented. In the published translation I read v├бrс╣гiс╣гс╣нhaiс╕е both with SavitarтАЩs sava├нс╕е, which has to be supplied, and with SomaтАЩs dh─Б╠Бmabhiс╕е, and tr. the latter slightly differently: тАЬthrough SomaтАЩs domains.тАЭ

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

Griffith

Cleanse us, God Savitar, with Three, O Soma, with sublimest forms,
Agni, with forms of power and might.

Grassmann

Durch die drei h├╢chsten Satzungen Gott Savitar, o Soma du, Durch Weisheit lautre, Agni, mich.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬMay the worshippers purify me, may the Vasus purify (me) by their acts; purify me, all you gods, (Agni) the intelligent purify me.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Worshippers: Or, the troop of gods, Indra and others

Jamison Brereton

Let the godly folk purify me; let the Vasus purify me with insight. O All Gods, purify me; o J─Бtavedas, purify me.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This last vs. of the purificatory spell is in a different meter (Anuс╣гс╣нubh) and makes reference to a larger variety of personnel - both often signs of finality.

22-27 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are a self-contained purificatory spell, with all vss. ending with an act. impv. belonging to the pres. pun─Б╠Бti: all 2nd sg. pun─лhi (naс╕е) except 22c pun─Бtu (naс╕е). The means of purification is, in the first instance, the filter (pav├нtra-), which of course literally means тАШinstrument (-tra-) of purificationтАЩ.

The pav├нtra is mentioned in vss. 22-25, with other, non-physical means of purification added (e.g., the sacred formulation vss. 23-24), with these means appropriate to the gods who wield them (SavitarтАЩs тАШimpulsionтАЩ [sav├б-] vs. 25). The first agent of purification is Soma Pavam─Бna himself (22), then Agni (23-24), then Savitar (25). The three - Soma, Agni, and Savitar - then appear together in 26, and in the final vs. (27) it opens out to all the divinities. The rhetoric is pretty flat, but the structure is a pleasing example of repetitive variation.

Griffith

May the Gods’ company make me clean, and Vasus make rue pure by song.
Purify me, ye General Gods; O Jatavedas, make me pure.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬLet us approach bearing the beloved loud-sounding youthful oblation fostered by our offerings.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Close to the dear youth who always excites wonder, the one growing strong through the poured offering,
have we come, bearing homage.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The hapax ─Бhut─л-vс╣Ы╠Бdh- could be either passive (as in the published translation, also Geldner) or act.

тАШstrengthening the oblationтАЩ (Renou). Scarlatta (514) considers both possibilities and opts, weakly, for the former. Either could certainly work in context. The long final vowel -t─л- is found only here, versus the standard ─Б╠Бhuti-. This can either be a metrical lengthening of the stem vowel, since *─Бhut─н-vс╣Ы╠Бdham, with 3 (or actually 4) light syllables in a row, would produce an unacceptable cadence. Or it can be an instr. sg. to the -ti-stem (see inconclusive disc. in Scarlatta.), which would clinch the interpr. of the cmpd as passive тАШstrengthened by the oblationтАЩ. I weakly favor the latter.

Jamison Brereton Notes

ItтАЩs not clear why we return to purely Soma vss. at this point, but the aoristic summary in 29 (├║pa тАж ├бganma b├нbhrato n├бmaс╕е тАЬup to him have we come bearing homageтАЭ) is another typical hymn-ender, summarizing the hymn that precedes.

Griffith

We with our homage have approached the Friend who seeks our wondering praise,
Young, strengthener of the solemn rite.

Zu dem lieben opfergest├дrkten J├╝ngling, der viel Geschrei macht, sind wir Huldigung bringend gekommen.

Dem lieben, wunderherrlichen dem J├╝ngling, der am Guss sich freut Sind Ehr’ erweisend wir genaht.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬMay the battle-axe of the foe destroy the foe; alone flow tous, bright Soma; (slay) the villain only, bright Soma.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The battle-axe: para┼Ыuс╕е = chedakaс╕е pavam─Бnaс╕е, as if Soma were metaphorically called a battle-axe and implored to turn his edge against foes only (tam eva ┼Ыatrum n─Б┼Ыayatu)

Jamison Brereton

The axe [=flame] of the one not to be stilled [=Agni] (has) disappeared. By purifying yourself bring it here, o god Soma,
so also the rat [=Rudra?], o god Soma.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, this vs. is extremely puzzling and my tr. and interpr.

are at best speculative. OldenbergтАЩs comments are useful in clearing away the detritus of the more fanciful interpr., but neither he nor the other standard comm. have cracked the code: see GeldnerтАЩs n. 30a with lit. The syntax and the sequence of events are quite straightforward: someoneтАЩs axe has disappeared (contra Grassmann, nan─Б┼Ыa must belong to тАШdisappearтАЩ, not тАШreachтАЩ; the тАШdisappearтАЩ association is now generally agreed upon), and Soma is asked to bring it back through his purification (the common idiom ─Б╠БтИЪp┼л). But whose axe? and why an axe in this context? and what does a rat have to do with it? To begin with the 1st question: most take al─Б╠Бyyasya as a PN (тАЬthe axe of Al─БyyaтАЭ), which saves trouble but doesnтАЩt help us in deciphering the verse. Grassmann derives it from тИЪl─л and tr. тАШsich nicht duckendтАЩ; Mayr (EWA s.v.) simply pronounces it тАЬunklar.тАЭ I suggest that it belongs to тИЪr─Б тАШcome to / be at restтАЩ, with an l-form like il├бyati (see Narten, тАЬVed. il├бyati тАж,тАЭ 1968; Jamison, -├бya-, 48-49, EWA s.v. R─А3 ). I would interpr. it as a gerundive like - p─Б╠Бyya- (тИЪp─Б both тАШdrinkтАЩ and тАШprotectтАЩ), as well as the more numerous extended stems of the type ┼Ыrav─Б╠Бyya-, whose -─Б- does not belong to the root. A parallel negated form (though again not to a ─Б-root) may be found in atas─Б╠Бyya- тАШunshakeableтАЩ; see comm. ad I.63.6. I suggest that al─Б╠Бyya- means тАШnot able to be brought to rest, not to be stilledтАЩ.

This may not seem to advance us very far, but this word must be evaluated in conjunction with para┼Ы├║с╕е, on which it depends. The para┼Ы├║- is frequently associated with Agni; cf., e.g., I.127.3, IV.6.8, and VI.3.4, in all of which Agni is compared to an axe. The other common word for axe, v─Б╠Б┼Ы─л-, is also characteristic of Agni: he carries it (VIII.19.23) and possesses it (v─Б╠Б┼Ы─лmant- X.20.6). AgniтАЩs axe must be his flame, and of course fire is always in motion, never still. I therefore suggest that al─Б╠Бyya- refers to Agni, the axe to his flame, and the p─Бda declares that his flame has disappeared or been lost. This may refer to the famous myth in which Agni runs away from his ritual duties and has to be coaxed back by offering him a better deal - or it may simply record a ritual disaster: the sacrificial fire has gone out. In any event Soma is taxed with bringing him back in p─Бda b.

If my interpr. of the first p─Бda is anywhere near correct, it pleases me to fancy that this is the first (very indirect) textual evidence to Para┼Ыu R─Бma.

The real puzzle in this vs. is p─Бda c, which presents ─Бkh├║m as an apparently parallel object to the para┼Ы├║- that Soma is supposed to bring back. Both Geldner and Renou find this relatively easy to deal with because they take it as a simile, marked with cid. But as I have noted in a number of places, there are no clear instances of cid as a simile marker, and here it is also not clear what similarity the poet might be trying to point to (that moles live hidden seems to be the best guess). My own suggestion is hardly better than this (if that). The ─Бkh├║is probably a тАШmole-like ratтАЩ since there are no true moles in the subcontinent (see Katz, JAOS 122 [2002], тАЬHow the Mole and Mongoose Got Their Names,тАЭ esp. 301-2). The word is found only here in the RV, which complicates the interpr., but it has become fairly common by middle Vedic. In particular, the ─Бkh├║- is associated with Rudra; already in VS III.57, TS I.8.6.1 the ─Бkh├║- is RudraтАЩs victim / portion at the Tryambaka ritual. I therefore think it is possible (no more than that) that Soma is being asked also to bring Rudra back, in the form of his totem animal. Of the few hymns dedicated in full or in part to Rudra (there are only 3 dedicated to him alone), 2 (I.43, VI.47) are Soma-Rudra hymns, so there is some association between the two gods, whose rationale unfortunately escapes me.

Why this vs. ended up in this hymn I have no idea - except as a composite hymn it may have attracted various vss. that were floating around, and since it addresses Soma and uses a standard Somian verb (─Б╠БтИЪp┼л), the hymn may have seemed as good a place as any to stash this vs. It clearly has nothing to do with the two Soma vss. that preceded nor with the added summary vss. that follow. It also appears to be a younger vs., given the -l-form and the non-RVic word ─Б╠Бkhu-.

тАЬHe who reads the hymns to Pavam─Бna, the essence (of the Veda) collected by the с╣Ыс╣гis, enjoys all (his food) purified, sweetened by M─Бtari┼Ыvan.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

M─Бtari┼Ыvan = Va+yu, because it breathes in the atmosphere, antarikс╣гe ┼Ыvasiti; the food is sweetened and purified by the purifying wind and the man eats it

Whoever studies the тАЬSelf-PurifyingтАЭ (verses), the sap assembled by the seers,
that one eats the whole purified (sap), sweetened by M─Бtari┼Ыvan. с│У

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the import of these two vss., see published introduction. They are obviously secondary additions to the hymn, promising great benefits to anyone who studies the previous vss. of the hymn.

The man who reads the essence stored by saints, the Pavamani hymns,
Tastes food completely purified, made sweet by Matarisvan’s touch.

Whoever studies the тАЬSelf-PurifyingтАЭ (verses), the sap assembled by the seers,

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the import of these two vss., see published introduction. They are obviously secondary additions to the hymn, promising great benefits to anyone who studies the previous vss. of the hymn.

Commentary IX.68-114

Griffith

Whoever reads the essence stored by saints, the Pavamani hymns,
Sarasvati draws forth for him water and butter, milk and meath.
